And it is suitable to use them, for the cupping instruments of children and women are not like those of men and their bodies. As for how to apply this cupping instrument with fire to the limb, it is that you ignite a small flame from a compact piece of wood or a small candle and place it in the center of the cupping instrument, which is fixed on the middle of the limb.
So that the fire rises upwards, towards the bottom of the cupping instrument, lest it burn the body of the patient. Then, it is placed on the limb, and the finger is placed on the hole we mentioned, until when we hold the cupping instrument, we do not remove our fingers.
The steam should exit through that orifice and the cupping glasses are placed on the affected area, then the lancet is brought to the surface and it is adjusted if it inclines towards it. As for the cupping glasses used in treating gout with water, there is no fixed groove nor orifice.
And they are used by filling them with water and placing them on the affected area only, and this is their form.
And these cupping glasses, whenever they were large, could hold a lot of water and were better.
Chapter 87.
On the treatment of abscesses.
The abscess, it is usually used in most cases on the organs where cupping glasses cannot be placed, either because of their smallness such as the lips and the teeth and the like, or because the organ is compact with flesh such as the fingers and the nostrils and the like.
And the method of using it is to suck out the abscess which is in the pure, clean water free from impurities, then leave it for a day and a night in the clean water until it dissolves and nothing remains in its hollow. Then the body is purged, either by phlebotomy or by cupping, then the affected area is massaged.
Until it becomes red, then it is treated with cupping glasses; if it becomes full and the cupping glasses can be applied to that place, it is more beneficial. Otherwise, the area is washed with vinegar and then with plenty of water, and it is bandaged, and if the blood flows after the abscess has fallen off, and that is a good sign, then a cold compress is applied to the area.
And it is placed from above until the bleeding stops; if there is a lot of blood, then a piece of zinc, or a small stone, or something similar from the stopping agents is applied to stop the blood, or an ointment made from the crushed leaves of the pomegranate tree is applied to the area.
Leave the ligature in place until the wound is closed, for if the blood stops flowing and it is necessary to re-ligate, do not re-ligate at that point unless there is no alternative, for if you release the ligature from re-ligating, wash the area with a lot of blood.
You insert a needle into the area until some blood comes out, then you place it; if you feel something in the blood, it will stick to the instrument. If you want to drop it, sprinkle some powder or salt or antimony on it, and it will drop onto the instrument.
Chapter Three.
On Suturing.
This chapter is also necessary and needed in the craft of medicine, which is suturing of fractured and broken bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter has been neglected by many physicians and laymen, and no one has written a book about it or even read a single letter about it.
And because of this, this art has become forgotten in our country, and I have not found a single knowledgeable person who can teach it. And I have benefited from it what I have benefited, through my prolonged reading of the ancient books and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted its knowledge from them, then I combined experimentation and practice throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you from that in this chapter all that encompasses my knowledge. And I have tested it after I had refined it for you, and freed it from the excess of prolivity, and shortened it to the utmost brevity, and made it clear to the utmost clarity, and illustrated for you in it many figures of the instruments that are used in it.
It is an increase in clarity, as I have done in the previous chapters, and there is no power except with God, the Exalted, the Great.
The First Section.
There are some general things and introductions about the art of bone setting, and how to present them. Before we start mentioning the fractured and dislocated limbs one by one, it is necessary to mention in this chapter some general statements and principles. You must first understand them and grasp their reality, you and whoever is zealous to learn this noble craft.
I say that if a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain, or fall occurs, it is necessary to hasten first to its treatment, or its healing, or all of them together. If it is not prevented from that, something will prevent it, such as weakness of the body or the one who suffered it being young or old, or the weather being extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold legumes and the meat of birds and game, and prevent hot drinks and fatty meats and excessive eating, and every food that fills the blood vessels with blood until, if he is certain of the inflammation, he does not hasten to apply a poultice to the affected area.
Then the patient should return to his first regimen, which is his usual habit. If he took a fractured bone in its proper place, it is necessary for him to nourish it with nourishments that are rich, fatty and sticky, such as Hippocras, rice, and meat broths, and milk of cow or goat. And eggs, and fat fish, and thick drinks, and so on. For with this regimen, the consolidation of the fracture will be faster and better. And know that if the fractured bones are in young men or old men, it is not possible for them to return to their natural state ever again.
Their bones are fragile and porous, and will not consolidate, but will rather be like the bones of very young children. However, nature will build upon the fractured bone from all its aspects something similar to a callus, which is thick and dense, and will hold it together until some of it adheres to another.
Some of them are tied together until they meet at the site of strength and firmness, just as if it were originally created that way, so that nothing of its effects is impaired.
For this reason, it is necessary to make the nourishment of the patient suitable and strong, and to avoid its being too little or too much, as we have said. And know that fractures vary in their types according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the thigh bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
And so, all the other members are different from one another. We will mention each type of fracture in its own chapter, explaining it in detail and distinguishing it from others. And a type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
For it may be a fracture that breaks into fragments without producing splinters, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone with splinters and protrusions, both embedded and not embedded. And the fracture may occur with a wound and a crack in the skin, and the fracture may be a simple one.
And each type of fracture has its own special trick for treating it, as will be mentioned in detail at its place. And what is known about bone fractures is their deviation and curvature, and their appearance to the senses, and their grating sound when you press on them with your finger.
So when there is no apparent deviation at the site, nor grating sound, nor sensation upon pressing on the bone with difficulty, and the doctor does not find much pain, it may not be a fracture, but rather a bruise or a slight crack, so it should not be treated with heavy pressure and bandaging.
But I will apply to it from the medicines that will be mentioned later, according to what suits the site. Then, I will treat it gently and gradually, and know that if the bone is broken and separated into two pieces without producing splinters, it is as if each part has moved away from its companion.
You should examine him from the moment you notice the symptoms and regulate them before they occur, for if they occur, leave him until the inflammation subsides, then treat it in whatever way you think fit, according to the severity of the disease and the patient's condition.
And know that treating a fracture and setting it is easier than one that has become compound, then it will be based on what the patient remembers, for if there are fragments in the bone, it is necessary to straighten the bent limb with the help of two assistants, whether it is an arm or a leg.
Put your hands, if the limb is small, or with pads, and with the help of a assistant, and place the limb in its natural position, until when you extend it, the broken bone will be straightened, then reduce those fragments to their places in whatever way you think fit according to the patient's condition and severity.
And beware of exerting force on the injured part with your hand, lest you cause pain or inflammation, and try to bring one bone edge to its fellow in the best position, and it is necessary at that time to feel them with your hand and examine them, for if you see something amiss, correct it and set it right according to your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and vigorous jerking, as many ignorant people do, which often leads to inflammation or putrefaction in the limb, as I have seen that happen many times due to their actions.
Then, after setting and straightening, immobilize the limb with bandages and splints, and warn the patient not to move it during his sleep, rest, or when he turns over, nor during all his movements, lest it becomes dislocated, and ensure that the position of the limb is one that prevents discomfort.
And that is when he feels, in the state of setting the limb, pain or discomfort, he should not move it to a different position until one where he does not feel discomfort, and ensure with that his setting is straight and even.
When a fracture occurs in a limb, and how to set the broken limb, I will describe it to you. You should know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness, and their treatment; for some of them are small like fingers, toes, and nails, and their likes.
It is necessary for the bandages to be wide and gentle, and not tight, because the strong ligature constricts the large limb and holds it from all sides with equal tension, without allowing any defect.
Then, after freeing yourself from immobilization, you should apply the suitable splint for that. There is a thin cushion on the site of the fracture, then you start wrapping the ligature around the site of the fracture itself three or four times according to what the limb deserves, and tighten your hand slightly with the ligature, then move it to the upper side of the site of the fracture.
You tighten less than you would for the broken site, then you loosen the wrap a little from the site of the fracture and relax the tension a little until you get something correct from the site, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken site.
You wrap, then you move the wrap to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action be in tightening the wrap and loosening it according to what we mentioned in wrapping the upper one first, then you put between the wraps some thin cushions or soft material that will help set the fracture.
If there is a fracture, and if not, do not put anything in it, then you wrap another bandage on it, and immobilize with these tight, secure wraps from your time, as long as there is no swelling or redness in the limb.
If there is swelling or redness, apply what will reduce that inflammation and let the swelling subside, then immobilize with the wraps at that time, and let the wraps be from stiff, straight, and well-made splints, either made of willow branches or being from the strong, straight wood of the Syrian olive tree.
You make a splint from the straight branch of a nut tree or a pomegranate tree or a walnut tree, and similar things that are strong enough for that, and you adjust each splint according to this form and shape.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the fractured limb, from its largeness to its smallness, the shape of the splint:.
Then you fasten the splints with another bandage according to the way you fastened the first one, then you tie above with tight ligatures according to what we mentioned about the fastening, and it is that the fastening should be on the site of the fracture more. And wherever the fastening is farther from the fracture, it is less.
And it is necessary that the ligatures be moderate in thickness and softness, and they should be from clean, moist bandages specifically, because if the ligatures were very thick like what I have seen some surgeons do, making their ligatures from folded strips of rough bandages, it is a great mistake.
Because the fastening with them is excessive and the soft ligatures also do not suit, because you do not achieve by fastening with them what you want, and it is not desirable that there be less than a finger's breadth between the splint and the splint.
So if the patient complains of the edges of the splints after fastening in the correct positions, put under them some soft padding or crumpled linen until it does not hurt him at all, and if the fracture is with a wound and there is a rupture in the skin, we deal with it separately.
And know that not every fractured limb needs to be splinted from the first day, and that is because if the limb is large, it should not be splinted until after five days or seven days or more, according to your fear of the occurrence of severe swelling.
This type of dressing that was used to treat the wounds on the head, shoulder and ribcage, is a general type of dressing that is abbreviated and treats the shoulder, and it is sufficient for most of the wounds, without being too tight or constricting.
And you should take the powder of the Rahi, which is the fine powder that adheres to the sides of the Rahi when it is moved during the process of grinding, and you should sift it in such a way that it is neither too coarse nor too fine, then you should use it.
Another type of dressing that treats the shoulder, knee and ribcage, is taken from the Msh, Lden, Aqqiya, Rsn, Mugth, and Sik, each weighing ten dirhams, and their mixture with five dirhams each, and twenty dirhams of Athl, and twenty dirhams of either Armenian or Roman clay. The whole is ground and mixed with water of Athl or egg whites if the mixture is too liquid, then this dressing is used, for it is good for setting bones quickly and it treats most people's dislocations.
Another type of dressing that is also beneficial for the shoulder and ribcage, is taken from Mugth, Msh, and white Khatmiy, each weighing ten dirhams, and their mixture with five dirhams each, Aqqiya six dirhams, Armenian clay twenty dirhams, the whole is ground finely and mixed with water or egg whites, and it is used.
A type of dressing for joints and slipping bones, which are dislocated from their positions, and it eases the severe pain associated with them, and sets the bone and shoulder in place. It is taken from the soaked Suffj, which is wrapped in a cloth and the Zait is melted, and it is applied to the affected area. And this dressing does not have any constricting power.
Here is the translation:
You want to analyze a section of the tumor, take from the basis of the cartilaginous and bony parts, and separate them with precision, taking care not to injure any part, and if the limb is not paralyzed, then anoint it with rose water or vinegar, and use it.
Another stronger type of bandage is used for analysis when a hard tumor occurs on the periosteum, taking from the basis of the cartilaginous and bony parts, and the ligament, and the King's melic, and merzengouch, and separating them with precision, each part by itself.
He bandages everything and anoints it with vinegar or sweet milk or rose water, all according to the temperature of the limb and its condition; and as for Aqrata, he did not mention in his book that it should be applied to the fractured limb during its setting.
The Kyriotikon, made from wax and oil, is nothing but a medium between the solidity and fluidity, and Galenous thought that it should be applied to the fractured limb during its setting with things that have a gentle heat, such as myrrh, storax, and labdan, and so on, as we mentioned.
A type of bandage is beneficial for ulcers and abscesses, taking marshmallows, and hemp, and human hair or bird's feathers, and cartilaginous parts, and dissolving them together, and then he bandages and anoints it, and as for the amount that should remain on the limb.
Then he should leave it, for if it does not happen with the abscess or ulcer or severe pain, and the fractured bone does not move from its position, do not touch it at all, even if it takes a long time, for if it happens in that position, there will be severe pain or a suppurating ulcer or swelling.
So hasten to remove the bandage at the time and do not delay it for a moment, then take a soft sponge or a linen cloth with a gentle touch, and dip it in warm water, and wash the place until the pain subsides and the abscess is removed, then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Here is the translation:
If the patient's condition has improved, I have seen that the swelling and redness and pain and puffiness and all the symptoms have disappeared completely and returned to normal, so I treated him as I did at first, for nothing of what we mentioned happened in the member.
You should not remove the dressing except after three, four, five or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days, all of that is according to what appears to you about the condition of the member as we have said, until the suppuration becomes certain and the flesh begins to consolidate over it.
You should also increase the compression more than the first compression, and you should also increase the consolidation of the food for the patient according to what has been mentioned previously, for I have seen that the place of suppuration was weak and fragile, more so than it should be, so I knew that the nutrition was impeding its reach.
So, change the dressing with a loose one each time you remove it, and let that be every three days, and reduce the compression slightly, for by this action the nutrition will reach the member quickly and it will heal rapidly; and as for what the ignorant people from the bandagers do, they suppurate the member another time.
For if they did not bandage properly as it should be, and they bandaged on unevenness, it is a grave error from their action and a great ignorance; and if it were correct, I would have mentioned it in the previous books and acted upon it, but I saw no effect from any of them regarding that. And the right thing is not to act according to it.
Chapter Two.
On Suppuration of Corrupt Matter in the Head.
The types of suppurating corrupt matter in the head are many and their forms are diverse, and their causes are varied; for among them is what occurs due to a blow with a sword, and it may be that the whole bone is broken until it reaches the dura mater beneath the bone. As the hammer acts on the wood.
And so this type of fracture is called a "qudumiyah" and it is that the cut of the sword only reaches part of the bone and does not penetrate to its end, and this type of fracture is called a "qla' mutlaqah". The wound from these two types of fractures may be large or small.
And of the fractures, there is one that is a bruise or a contusion and it occurs due to a blow with a stone or a fall onto a stone or something similar. And this fracture may be either a superficial one that approaches the membrane beneath the bone, or one that is on the surface of the bone.
And the wound from these two fractures is also either large or small. And of the fractures, there is one that is hidden in the bone at the level of the hairline, and it is a subtle crack that occurs.
And so this type of fracture is called a "shu'uriyah". And there is a fracture that occurs due to a fall or a blow with a stone and the like, which drives the bone surface inward and becomes deformed in the place where it lies, just as it would exhibit if a copper vessel were struck with a blow and a part of it were driven inward.
And this is more likely to occur in the moist, spongy bone, such as the skulls of young children. And it may be that all these types of fractures have fragmented or unfragmented pieces. We will discuss the treatment of each of them in its place, and we will become familiar with all these types of fractures through examination.
And you examine them with the probes and remove the putrid, filthy flesh from them. And as for the "shu'uriyah" type, it is recognized by examining the bone and scraping and rubbing it with the file, for then the fracture appears black. And as for treating the fracture, you first consider the symptoms of the patient.
For if you see among his symptoms what indicates clear signs of fear, such as trembling, extension, and wandering of the mind, and interruption of speech, and anxiety, and severe, piercing, and inflamed eyes, then do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is likely to occur with these symptoms in most cases without exception.
If you find a patient who is not bothered by his condition and has submitted to the treatment, then take care of him at that time, because if the injured part had been treated from the beginning, it would have been during the winter days.
You should extract the bone before the 14th day in any case, and if it is during the summer days, you should hurry to extract the bone before the 7th day, lest the material under the bone putrefies from the membrane, so those symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and it is with a severe concussion and depression, you should cut off the affected part of the injury, as I described to you, which is to trephine the skull of the injured person.
And uncover the bone on whichever side is convenient for you, according to the shape of the wound and what is hidden from the patient, for when you uncover the bone, you will find a flow of blood or a warm abscess, so be prepared for that with what is necessary.
And it is to stuff the area with a dressing soaked in wine and oil, and to clean the wound until the abscess settles and the bleeding stops, then you take care of setting the bone and extracting it, and that will be on one of two sides of the operation.
As for the single aspect, it is to cut the bone with a gentle curved incision, and this is its form:.
Then you use another incision after this one, slightly above it, and this is its form:.
Then you also use another incision, also above the second one, and it is necessary for you to have several different incisions, some of which are above others and some shorter than others, and they should be extremely sharp at their edges, made from Indian steel or good quality iron.
And use the file to strike on the incision, so that you don't displace the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is better to perforate around it before using the incisions with the instruments you have named non-piercing and I have named them non-piercing.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the bone of the skull to what is beyond it, so that the periosteum has a straight line beneath its base, outlining it with a circle or small ring, preventing it from penetrating and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is desirable for you to take from these periosteum several straight lines, each one of which is suitable for the thickness of that bone, until you have prepared a sufficient amount for each skull, with a straight edge in length and width, according to the thickness of that skull, and there are three types of this. From the large periosteum, medium, and small:.
And as for how to make a circle around the fractured bone, it is that you place the periosteum on the bone and press it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has been reached, then you transfer the periosteum to another position, and you make a space between each circle according to the thickness of the rods or their likes.
Then you cut with the forceps what is between each two circles, and do that with great skill as much as you can, as we have mentioned, until you saw away the bone, either with your hand or with another instrument from some of the tools that I have prepared for that.
Like the scoop and the delicate forceps, and it is necessary to beware with all caution that you do not touch the periosteum or the instrument to anything of the suture, for if you have sawed the bone and separated it from the suture, and it was adhering to it and brought it out, it is necessary that you should scrape.
And you smooth out the roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the forceps, but it is necessary that it be more delicate and finer than the other forceps, for if there remain some small bones and fragments, you take them with skill using what you have of instruments.
Then we treat the wound with the ointments and remedies that will be mentioned, and as for the other aspect of the work, it is an easy task, far from the difficulty that Galen and his admirers have described, and this is what he says. It is suitable for you to start by exposing the part of the bone from the site where the fracture is most prominent and most known, until when you have exposed that part, you will have formed an idea about this section of the broken bone, and this is its picture.
The broken part of it is smooth and does not cut anything, and the straight part of it is in its two ends as you see, to be the broken part leaning on the splint, and the surface of the broken bone is then struck from one side.
With a small saw, until you cut through all of the bone with ease, as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, in which no frightening accident happens to the patient, and if the healer is among the ignorant people, he would be misled, and even if he was experienced.
For if there remains a piece of the bone attached to the membrane at some places, you dissect it away from the surface of the broken bone itself and free it from it with ease, so that it is freed from it without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone did not reach the membrane, and what was cut off from the bone was its surface, and there remained roughness and fine fragments, it is suitable that you should scrape away that roughness and remove those fragments with a gentle file, which has taken various different forms, so that you can use them in every place, and adjust and correct according to what the work guides you.
And the same broken bone and its shape, and it is suitable that you should use in scraping first the most prominent part of the bone with those files, then use afterwards a finer one, and do not cease to do that repeatedly until you reach using the finest and most delicate ones.
As for the small, fine, and delicate incisions, it is necessary to use each one according to what leads to its success, which is a thing that does not escape anyone who has a minimum of skill in this craft, and stops at what we have written and clarified concerning the large wounds.
For if the periosteum remains exposed when you extract the bone, it is necessary to take a piece of cloth equal in size to the wound, and soak it in oil and fat, and place it on the mouth of the wound; then you take another piece of cloth, either triangular or quadrilateral. And soak it in serous discharge and oil and fat, and place it on the first piece of cloth; and make that with as little pressure as you can, so that you do not burden the wound; then you use a wide bandage from above, and do not constrict it except to the extent that it holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the painful inflammation subsides, then you remove it and use afterwards the medicaments that are beneficial, such as the juice of the willow, and the powder of the crust of the horn, and the powder of the knotweed, and the Zar, and their like; and it is that you make a poultice from these medicaments.
And apply to the wound as it is, and with the bandage, so use in that a medicine that cleanses without irritating, and direct your effort at the time of treating the wound to be clean, not containing harm from fat or pus, nor letting the discharge gather in it.
For if the discharge gathers on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and destroys it, and as a result, a severe affliction occurs on the patient; and it may appear in the sutures of the skull when the bone is exposed, or even more so if its treatment is neglected.
If you see blackness on its surface, it is a sign that the patient has the symptoms we mentioned, so know that he is in danger, without a doubt; and if the blackness occurred because of a medicine applied to it, and there was power in that medicine to produce that blackness.
Take a part of honey and three parts of rose oil, mix them well, then add a layer of kohl, then apply it to the wound, and treat it with the required treatments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Treat the nose if it is fractured.
Know that the nose does not break unless the upper jaw or one of them is broken, because they are strong bones, and it is unlikely to break, but rather it will be subject to bruising, displacement, and fracture.
If one of the jaws is broken, it is necessary to insert a small finger into the nostril and adjust the fracture from the inside with your finger, while supporting from the outside until the nose takes its natural shape, and be gentle so that you do not cause any harm to the surrounding area or jaw.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, it is necessary to adjust it with a rod inserted gently, and if the fracture is on both sides, do the same, and do not delay in treating it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, and if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, when the severe inflammation has subsided, then insert a piece of cloth into the nostril, taken from a clean new cloth, if the fracture is on one side, or insert two pieces if the fracture is on both sides.
And let the cloth be inserted gently to the extent that it fills the nostril, and some of the surgeons among our predecessors mentioned that one should grease the cloth with butter and change it every day, but I did not find that beneficial, rather it is necessary to grease the cloth with egg white mixed with powdered rose water.
Then leave the cloth until the bone is established and the cartilage is consolidated, and you may insert into the nose, in the place of the cloth, two or three reeds of the cane, wrapped with a clean cloth, so that it holds the fracture of the nose firmly and does not allow the jaw to move.
This is a necessary thing if you want to make it and if you want to make the sutures, for then you will present it to the nose during your operation and stitch the nose with the qirootiy or with a straightened thread in a circular way or with a twisted thread from the rhinocarion.
For if it does not present and the nose is broken, then you should stitch it from outside with a thick suture and thin threads of Kendra; we have found that the white of an egg is beneficial, then you put a compress on it for a day or two, and do not bind the nose with a thing of biteness; for if you break the bone of the nose, it will be a small fracture.
Or you should make an incision on it and extract it with the instrument that is suitable for it, then you should close the incision and bandage it with what sticks and adheres from the suitable medicines; for if a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with a dressing and use nasal pipes of silver. Until he recovers.
The Fourth Chapter.
Treating the fracture of the lower jawbone.
If the fracture of the lower jawbone is not accompanied by a wound, it will be easy to treat, for if the fracture is only on the outside and does not break into two pieces and does not sink inwards, then its treatment will be simple.
It is necessary that if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the middle finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth and likewise, if the fracture is on the left side, you should insert the middle finger of the right hand and raise the fragment of the fracture from inside outward.
And you should hold the other fragment from outside the bone, adjusting it, for if the fracture of the jaw has broken into two pieces, it is necessary to use a press from the two sides on its straightness until its adjustment is complete; and if there is displacement in the teeth.
And separate what you have collected from it, so that it remains with a thread of gold or silver or an ivory pin, then place on the fractured skull a circular clamp, and put on it a thick, firm, padded dressing, or a piece of felt like a shoe sole, suitable for the length of the fracture. Then bind it from above, according to what you find convenient for binding and adjusting its tightness until it does not become loose, and order the patient to avoid exertion and violence and to make his food light and easy to digest, for if you think that something has changed in his appearance or condition, then hasten to relieve him on the third day.
Then bind it from above, according to what you find convenient for binding and adjusting its tightness until it does not become loose, and order the patient to avoid exertion and violence and to make his food light and easy to digest, for if you think that something has changed in his appearance or condition, then hasten to relieve him on the third day. Then heal what you have fastened with a plaster of rose-water or egg-white or fine powder, after removing the clamp from it and placing on the dressing a light, gentle compress; as long as that dressing remains on it and does not change for the better condition of the bone, leave it alone.
Do not uncover it until it heals and the fracture is consolidated; for it often takes three weeks for this fracture to consolidate, if a fever or severe inflammation appears during that time, then use what we have mentioned repeatedly to calm it down until that inflammation subsides.
And if the fracture is with a wound that has a splinter of bone, and it has become separated from the main bone, then be gentle in extracting those splinters according to your ability to remove them with the instrument; if the mouth of the wound is narrow, then widen it with the dilator as much as you need.
Then, if you have extracted those splinters and none of them remain, stitch the mouth of the wound if it is wide, and if not, then apply one of the medicaments that are suitable for that. And bandage the wound until it heals.
The Fifth Chapter.
On setting a broken rib.
The fracture of the clavicle is more frequent from the inner aspect than its outer extremity, and its union takes place in one of three ways: either it unites and consolidates with two fragments without any splinters, which is easier to reduce.
And sometimes it happens that splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to reduce. And sometimes the fracture occurs with a wound, and the operation is performed if the fracture is not with a wound, by placing two assistants, one of whom holds the injured limb, and the other stretches the neck towards the other side.
Then you align the fracture with your fingers until it takes its proper shape, without any protuberance or curvature. If you need to apply more pressure, it is proper to place under the armpit a cushion made of soft leather or a pad.
And its bone is according to your need, and you stretch and elevate the clavicle, and press the cushion with your hand until the fracture is aligned properly. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outward in order to set it in its proper place, it is proper to let the arm lie on a pillow.
And you place a moderate-sized pad under the armpit on the bone, and you press the assistant's hand downwards until the clavicle's bone, which is in the depression, rises above, and then you correct the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, it is proper to open up and extract that splinter gently, if the splinter is stuck in the bone, you should make an incision with one of the instruments I have prepared for that purpose. After you have placed under the clavicle the instrument that protects the pleura, which is an instrument made of wood or iron, and this is its shape.
It resembles a spoon, without any curvature, and its width is according to the size of the bone, whether large or small, and likewise its length, depending on what you need for the operation, and it has two ends, one of which is wider and the other narrower.
If the fracture of the wound that you made while extracting a splintered bone and you encountered inflamed swelling, then gather the edges of the wound with sutures. If the fracture is small or the inflamed swelling is slight, then close the wound with the fracture and apply a compress in proportion to the size of the fracture.
If there is inflamed swelling, then apply a compress of rose water and vinegar or wine, and place it on the bone. If the bone was broken without a wound or fracture, then it is necessary to apply a poultice made from dust of roses with egg whites. And you put a soft poultice on it, then place a pad under the armpit if needed, then take a long splint that is slightly curved and apply a bent compress on the poultice and the bone, then take a plaster from a thin sheet. It should be three fingers in width and also in length, then insert it into the fracture, then insert that fracture with the splint at the place of the poultice which lies on the site of the fracture, then bind the poultice to the fracture as it is wrapped around. And fasten it around the neck and under the sound armpit and under the ailing armpit, and wrap it repeatedly on each side, and as I saw that the binding controls the fracture perfectly, which is what does not hide from you.
And pay attention to all of it so that the splint does not slide away from the fractured bone. And for that, it is necessary to inspect the patient every day, so whenever you find the bandage relaxed and see that the splint has shifted, then correct it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on his back and place a small cushion under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
Let the patient's limb be elevated above his breast, for elevating the fracture of the clavicle is by raising the shoulder or binding his arm to his neck, and do not remove the bandage unless an incident occurs at the site, such as a blow or inflammation, until twelve days.
If you have seen a fracture in the skull and there is a wound for that. Apply a bandage to the fracture and leave it until it begins to form a scab, and it will be strengthened more in 18 days. And it may be that with some people it is less than that.
Chapter Six.
The fracture of the shoulder. It is rare that the shoulder fractures in its thin part, but rather it fractures at its joints. When it fractures, it does not fracture at a specific spot or in its middle, and this is known by feeling, so according to what shape the fracture takes.
Reduce it and return it to its natural shape by all means possible, then place on the site of the fracture a dressing of rose water and egg white and vinegar, and put above it a compress of warm linseed oil, then apply a wide bandage from linen.
Bind it to the size of the shoulder, all of it or a little wider, and if there is swelling under the bandage at a site of the shoulder, then soothe that swelling with vinegar until the bandage settles in place, then fasten from above with a long binder.
Fasten it firmly and tighten the bandage so that it does not move from its place, and loosen the binder every day; whenever the binder becomes loose, tighten it and adjust the bandage if it moves from its place. And let the patient lie on his sound side, and the shoulder will mend in 20 days.
20 days or 25 days, and if you complete this regimen, remove the binder and you will be safe; for it is one of the bones that does not displace, and its fragments do not unite. If a splinter of bone protrudes and was hidden under the skin, then split open and extract it. What we mentioned in the fracture of the cranium from the two pointed instruments for the sharp wound, if something of that happens.
Chapter Seven.
On the Fracture of the Ribs.
The ribs may fracture in their middle and slightly, but what is exposed of that is more likely to fracture and become comminuted. And among its symptoms, if it fractures in the middle, it inclines downwards and becomes very painful, with difficulty in breathing.
And perhaps the patient may hide it, and the fracture is not apparent to the senses, but he will place his hand on his back, and bend his shoulder, and then flexes his elbows, and collects his arms from the two sides, and adjusts them in a way that makes it possible.
And agree that the shape of the bone returns to what is desired, then place upon it a compress and a bandage, and apply above it a splint made from a thin plate or from a piece of willow or similar materials, after wrapping it in a clean cloth. Then adjust it by binding it to the fractured bone so that it does not move, and pass the bandage around in a circular fashion to the back, passing it several times and tightening it firmly. Then check the bandage at every time, and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it.
And if the necessity calls for its removal when it appears at the site of the wound or the abscess, then hasten to remove it and lift the compress, and correct what has appeared from that by a method of treatment. Then reapply the compress if you see fit. And keep him bandaged tightly until he recovers.
Chapter Eight.
On the fracture of the limbs, know that the limbs are prone to fracture in the strong and thick areas close to the joints and their extremities, since they are cartilaginous and it is difficult to detect it by feeling with the fingers during examination.
Here is the translation:
The fracture that is exposed to one who suffers from it because of the weakness of the bone, and also shows fatigue, cough, and spitting of much blood, and this is a difficult treatment, for the ancients have tried many remedies in it.
Some of them have said that it is necessary to make the digestive organs absorb what produces flatulence and gases, so that the belly may be inflated and expanded, and the fracture may be pushed outward, and we reject this because it would not be a cure for the hot tumor, if it has not occurred, and if it has occurred, it increases it and makes it stronger.
Some have said that a cupping glass should be placed on the affected area, and then suction is applied with force, which is similar to cauterization, but it is feared that the cupping glass may attract the pus to the affected area due to its weakness. Some have said that it is necessary to cover the affected area with a cloth that has been soaked in hot oil.
And a plaster is made between the splints until it is filled, so that the bandage may be straight if it is wrapped around in circular fashion. Then, the digestive organs are treated with dietary and medicinal treatments, for the relaxation of the digestive organs is a severe matter that cannot be delayed. And the bone was fractured in a way that was damaging, and we feared for the digestive organs, so it is necessary to make an incision at the affected area and expose the broken rib, then we insert the instrument that preserves the pleura, which is advanced and described, and we cut the bone with precision and reset it.
Then we gather the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures and treat it with medications until it heals. If a hot tumor appears during that time, we quickly apply a cold compress on the affected area and treat the digestive organs with what alleviates the tumor from the inside as well.
And he rests on the side that is most comfortable for him, until he recovers.
The Ninth Section.
On the surgical treatment of the hernia in the back and nape of the neck.
As for the vertebrae of the nape of the neck, if it suffered a fracture and there was little that could be presented to it, and most of what could be presented to it was a blow, and likewise the vertebrae of the back also. So if that happened to someone and you wanted to know whether he would recover or not. So look, if you saw that his hands were paralyzed and weakened, and he was deadened and could not move them or control them, and if you poked them with a needle or pricked them with a point, he would not feel it and would not find pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most cases, for he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the poking and pricking, then know that the marrow of the bone is sound and the patient will recover with treatment. If a hernia in the back occurred like that and you wanted to know whether it would also recover or not.
So look at his legs, if you saw that they were paralyzed and the same thing happened to them as happened to his hands. Then, if he leaned on his back, gas and wind would emerge without his will, and if he lay on his stomach, urine would emerge without his will. And if he lay on his back and wanted to urinate but could not, then know that he is dying, so do not bother with his treatment. For if nothing of that appeared, the matter was lighter, and the treatment for what happened from that is to apply cooling compresses to the hot tumor.
By applying to the affected area a medicated plaster made with rose oil, either alone or with roasted egg yolks, applying it three times a day until the hot tumor subsides. Then apply one of the soothing cooling bandages to the area, and bind it with a bandage, and order him to rest and settle.
He should not sleep on the side where he does not feel pain until he recovers, for if a fracture occurred in the bone and a splinter or a shard has separated from it, it is necessary to make an incision on him and extract that bone. Then gather the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with medicated bandages until he recovers. If the fracture is at the end of the bone of the forearm and it is a weakness in the wrist, it is necessary to insert the little finger from the left hand into the palm.
And set the fractured bone with the other hand according to what is possible and adjust it, then apply a compress and a bandage. If you need that, then fasten it.
If you feel a splintered fracture in it, make an incision on it and extract it, and treat the wound with treatment as described until he recovers.
The Tenth Chapter.
Treating a fracture of the hip.
If the bones of the hips are fractured, then their fracture is that they become dislocated in their extremities and separate in length and incline inward, and it shows externally at the joint and causes pain and numbness in the leg from that side.
And treat it by passing your hand over it until you feel the fracture, and see how its shape is. If the fracture is only in its extremities, then set that fracture according to what appears to you from the adjustment, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is in the length or has incline inward, then place the joint on its belly until you feel that you have treated that fracture. If you have adjusted it, apply a compress to it, then put a splint made of wood or leather and fasten it tightly.
Do not fear to move the fracture or removing the splint, and adjust the bandage with what fills it, until it takes a firm hold on its straightness. And order him to sleep on his back or on his sound side, for if he has an acute feverish inflammation.
Remove the bandage and splint until the inflammation subsides and the swelling goes down, then return to the splint and tighten it as needed. If there is a break in the bone or a crack from its edges, do not remove it.
And do not touch it, but rather wrap it from the outside as we mentioned, and leave the splint until it heals.
Chapter 11
On setting a fractured bone.
The arm is from the elbow to the head of the shoulder, and if it breaks, set it on one of two sides, one being that you take a straight rod, smoothed and curved in this shape:
And bind it with two bandages at its ends, then suspend it from a elevated place, and let the patient sit on a stool, then put his broken arm on the rod until it becomes straight in the middle of the curve of the rod, then tie something heavy above it.
Or let a servant extend it downwards, then the doctor wraps the fracture with his hands together until the fracture is set as needed; and the other way is that the patient lies on his face and ties his hand to his neck with a bandage.
Then order two servants to hold, one of them holding above the fracture with his hand and the other holding below it, and each of them pulling towards his side; and if you want the pull to be stronger, tie under the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and let each of the two servants pull towards their sides.
If the fracture is near the shoulder, it is necessary to put one bandage under the armpit and another below the fracture towards the elbow; and likewise if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary to put the bandage on that place and on the elbow itself.

When the abscess is formed, open it, and clean its surface so that you can apply a dressing to the wound; then take a piece of new cloth and apply it to the dressing, and bring the arm together over itself and place your hand openly on the bandage.

The Twelfth Chapter.
On the Fracture of the Upper Arm Bone.
The arm consists of two bones, and they are called the Zhengdin; one is small and it is the one that extends to the elbow, and the other is large and it is situated underneath the small one from the bottom up; sometimes the large Zhengdin breaks alone or the small one alone, and sometimes they both break together.
When the small upper Zhengdin breaks, its treatment is easy and its healing is quicker; and when the lower Zhengdin breaks, its fracture is severe and its healing is difficult. If both bones break together, it was as if the bone that broke was the small upper Zhengdin.
It is necessary for the doctor, when treating him, to make his bandage gentle at first so that he can get used to it; if the large Zhengdin is the one broken, it is necessary to make the bandage stronger; and if both Zhengdins are broken, it is necessary to make the bandage even stronger.
It is necessary to put the shape of the hand in a position during treatment and to bandage it, stretched out on a pillow, with the elbow above the level of all the fingers, and the wrist below them, and the little finger resting on itself.
And let the pillow be placed under it at an elevation so that the little finger does not become bent.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, or with his hand, and another servant extends it from above, like that; then the doctor sets the bone in shape until he puts it in the best possible form; if there are fragments of bone broken off, they are treated.
Here is the translation of the excerpt:
Take the treatment from there, and apply it at first to the prominent vein, and let the sharp instrument be inserted through the perineum, between the thigh and the hip joint, and press it firmly until the tumor settles.
Then insert the perineum instrument, with its blunt point, through the dust of the rhad, with the whiteness of the egg, and then move the needles, and let the needle that is placed on the aching spot be slightly raised and strengthened, and know that the number of needles in the arm will be more in most cases.
