The 13th Chapter.
On the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
The wrist and finger joints are rarely affected by fractures, but rather they are often affected by abundant contusions, so when a fracture or contusion occurs in the wrist.
One should place the injured person in a sitting position and his arm should be placed on a level surface.
Then he should extend his arm and stretch out the broken bone, and the physician should adjust it until it is in its proper position. Then he should apply a compress and a splint, if there is no inflammation or redness. Then he should apply a plaster cast from above, according to the size of the site.
And he should bandage it with a soft cloth, if the fracture is at the lower end towards the palm of the hand, so make a circular shape from the cloth and instruct the injured person to grasp it with his broken hand, then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the plaster cast be from a single piece of cloth, so that the cloth is not bent with the bending of the entire hand, and tie it as needed. If the fracture is on the outside, one should make a plaster cast from above and another from below in the palm of the hand, so that the hand remains open and straight.
Then he should apply the bandage as the hand is turned and interlace between the fingers with a strip, if the fracture is in one of the finger joints. If it is in the thumb, adjust it as needed, then bind it with the palm, and if you want to make a small standing plaster cast to hold the fracture in place.
Do not move if the fracture is in the middle fingers such as the third or fourth or fifth, but splint it with a small straight splint and bind it with the adjacent finger to make it stable, or bind all of them together on the wrist.
As we mentioned in the case of the arm and its treatment, and after the swelling has subsided, you should apply what is necessary when the pain recurs, and describe it as it happens.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of fractures of the thigh.
The thigh bone often fractures and becomes dislocated because it is bent forward and backward, and its treatment is by applying a bandage above the fracture and another below it, with the patient lying on his face.
Then, he should stretch each bandage to its direction on the basis of this, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; and if it is near the base of the thigh, it is necessary to apply a soft bandage, either from a pillow or something similar.
At the base of the thigh, towards the groin, the bandage should be placed above and the other below the fracture; and if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee so that it presses from below. Then, the doctor will align the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural shape.
And the bone will reunite nicely, so when that happens, it is necessary to remove the bandage and apply pressure; if no swelling or inflammation occurs in the limb, then wait until the inflammation subsides, and then return to its treatment.
And it is necessary to apply a rigid and wide splint on the fracture for two or three days, and then leave what remains; then, apply a splint to the leg until the ankle becomes stable at the base of the limb, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, and fix the ends of the thread from above on both sides.
Then you should place a bandage on the thigh and calf, leaving what remains of the surgical dressing. Then you should apply to the thigh at the site of the fracture itself several layers of dressing, and make one layer over the bone of the calf. Then, fasten the splint that is between the thigh and calf with a linen roller to straighten it.
Then, start to apply strong bandages from the middle over the site of the fracture, three or four layers. And each time you wrap with a bandage from the site of the fracture, make your tightening weaker and more gentle.
Then, direct the end of the thread that I had inserted between the thigh and calf to what is above from the layers of dressing, then pass the end of the thread down until it reaches the ankle of the leg, and fasten it there also, with the ends of the dressing on the other side.
Do not loosen the bandage from its position, then leave the tightening on it as long as it does not harm the limb, nor become inflamed, nor swollen, nor putrefy, and so on. If something of that happens, then hasten to release it and correct what has happened from that, all according to what we have mentioned previously.
If there is a splinter in the bone that has entered, it is necessary to remove it if you can, otherwise, make an incision and extract it, and treat the wound with what we have mentioned previously until it heals. This will fix the fracture of the thigh without adding the calf with dressings.
As we mentioned in the arm and forearm, except that its splinting like this does not combine with it for the patient's comfort, and if you splinted it alone without adding the calf to it, then it is inevitable that its owner will always be lame.
Be aware that the thigh is stabilized in approximately 50 days, or a little more or less, all of which depends on the difference in temperament and other conditions.
The Fifteenth Chapter.
On the fracture of the cranium.
The cranium fracture is rare, and it often suffers many contusions, and if it does suffer a fracture, then it will be either a split or a depression in its parts, and that will happen with or without an wound.
And you should rely on sensation for that, and set it with the fingers so as to restore what is dislocated from its parts, until they reunite and adjust according to the way of setting and alignment, then apply the dressing and apply a round splint if you need to.
And you should tighten from above with a suitable compression, then observe all the conditions that we described in other fractures, such as inflammation and its likes, by opposing each obstacle with what is suitable for it until he recovers.
The Sixteenth Chapter.
On the setting of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh has two bones, one thick and called the femur, and the other thin and called the patella, and they are susceptible to types of fracture that affect the bones of the arm. And therefore, its setting is like the setting of the arm, and the work is one, for if the fracture is in both bones, it will displace the thigh to all directions, and if the fracture is in the thin bone, it will displace the thigh forward, and if the fracture is in the thick bone and occurs from below, that is something that cannot be hidden from you.
So use extension and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the thigh is a comminuted one with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and you should exert your effort in setting it.
And in the thigh, there is something additional to the arm, which is that when you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two sticks of cedar wood or others like them from the palm trees, which are used to straighten the rafters that are placed between the grooves of the boards.
Choose from them what is slightly thick and not from the thin ones, and let its length be along the length of the leg from the knee to the ankle, then wrap each one with two bandages along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the other side.
And let it be from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the two fascia in three places, at the extremities and the middle, for with this bandage the leg will be steady and not lean to the right or left, and it will be strong, and a splint made of wood along the length of the leg can be used.
And it is put in place to prevent movement, and it is most necessary to do that with a fracture if there is a specific wound with it, then inspect the leg every day and pay attention to any sign of swelling or inflammation or anything else. When something of that happens, treat it with what is necessary until it heals, and the bone of the leg will be restored in thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes.
The ankle is not prone to fractures of the splint, and the bones of the leg are prone to fracture, and the toes also rarely have fractures, but rather they are exposed to bruising in most cases.
If the bones of the leg are exposed to fracture and you find that those bones have overlapped each other, then put the injured foot on the ground standing upright like a pillar, then you should put your foot on what has protruded from those bones and press them in.
Until they return to their places, then take the padding and the bandage from above and place them under the sole of the foot like a small cushion that has two flat surfaces, then bind the foot tightly after wrapping it with bandages and other things needed for it.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage, and you will find the bone in its proper position, whether it was fractured or dislocated. If some fingers are bent, straighten them and adjust them according to what I described to you about adjusting fingers of the hand.
Then apply a splint to the fractured finger along its length, and let it be slightly elevated above it. Then place under the foot the board that I described to you, and fasten it firmly, especially if the break was in two or three or more bones.
Put a splint on each finger, proportional to its size, wrapped in a linen bandage, and fasten the board at the bottom of the foot, with each head of the board protruding outside the sole of the foot to hold it in place properly.
And beware that you do not forget to observe all that we mentioned to you in other fractures from the symptoms that we mentioned, and deal with each symptom according to what is suitable.
The 18th Chapter.
On the fracture of the clavicle in women and the collar bone, and mentioning the man.
When a woman's collar bone is fractured, put her in a recumbent position, then incline her slightly towards her back, and hold it from behind, then fasten the clavicle with bandages until it is fully covered and becomes like a ball in her chest.
Then, gently move the woman and raise her back slightly, for that bandage will come out until it becomes like a ball at the chest opening, and the fracture of the bone will be restored. Then, put a pillow on her back, so when she wants to cough, remove the bandage with care until she coughs.
And return it to the shape you initially formed, then return it to its original position. Do that for seven days or so, and it will be restored. If you fear that a pus collection may occur, insert a tube into her mouth and introduce the entire collection into her chest.
Then, you should insert the tube with force until it reaches the interior of the anus, then the fracture will return to its place. Then, bandage with cotton and apply a regimen as we have described until he recovers. And if the bone of the hip joint of a man or woman is fractured, it should be treated in this manner.
And you should treat it as we have described for the thigh bone, and do not hide from yourself the correctness in these strange fractures that rarely occur and are not well-known to those who have some experience, and he who understands this book of mine will be excellent, because the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
You should proceed correctly in its treatment and bandage, and understand. And if a man's thigh is fractured, take a poultice of colycynth and insert it into the anus, then apply a dressing made of wool and bind it with a ligature, and leave it for three days or thereabouts until he recovers.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
On the treatment of fractures of bones when they are with wounds.
It is necessary for one who has a fracture with a wound, and it is not hidden if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the arm bone, that you should hasten to treat it from its time, so that you may be helped by the conditions of treatment as we have mentioned.
If the wound is pouring out blood, it is necessary to hasten to its treatment by applying a hemostatic powder, if you do not have anything else, then proceed to treat the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If there is no severe inflammation, and if severe inflammation occurs, then postpone treatment until the ninth day, until the severe inflammation subsides. Do not touch it on the third and fourth days, for it will bring about bad consequences.
If the fractured bone is protruding out of the skin, it is necessary to reduce it and set it in place with your hand gently and gradually. And if you are unable to reduce and set it in place with your hand, use this instrument, which is a tool made of iron, its length being about seven or eight fingers. The Treatment of Wounds
Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the need of treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be slightly tightened so they do not loosen when pressure is applied during operation.
And let its extremity be sharpened with a curve at its tip, and its upper part be towards the tightness, and from its middle to its lower part be very thin, and this is its shape.
And it is called by the Greeks ' ', a small instrument; it is necessary that its sharpened curved extremity be placed on the fractured bone, and pressed upon it gently until, when the bone returns to its position and settles somewhat, the fragments of the fracture are adjusted to each other.
If the fractured extremity is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, it is necessary to cut through that bone until the instrument can grasp it firmly; if you are unable to return the bone to its position as we described previously, then cut it according to the sections we mentioned.
Or, if you are able to do so, extend the bone with one of the forceps in a way that is possible for you; then scrape off what remains of the roughness and thin skin from the bone. If you have returned the bone and found it still painful and very tender after its return, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so; for you will have achieved a great benefit and relieved the patient of a great pain. If you have completed your treatment of the bone, apply a dressing with a black, sticky bandage in the summer, and do not put anything on the wound that would cause it to become inflamed.
So that putrefaction and corruption do not occur, then use the bandages when you have finished treating the bone, and leave the wound uncovered, applying the compress with a suitable pressure according to the size of the wound.
Beware extremely of tightening the bandage with the splint, for many have done that and caused harm to their patients, resulting in either death or gangrene or abscesses, and let not your tightening be contradictory to the tightening of other splints.
If the wound is weak or a large wound and you fear some severe complications that we have described, and you find pain in the affected area, then it is not advisable to put splints on it and make wrappings with stiff linen at the place of the splints, and tighten them with it.
If, after a day or two, you see the wound has started to suppurate, then remove the linen that was applied with the dressing, and use the lancet and medications that are part of our custom to treat wounds, such as the quartic ointment and its like, and it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every morning and evening until it becomes swollen and heals, and it is necessary to position the limb in a way that the pus flows down easily. If many days have passed for the wound and it has not become inflamed nor has the pus ceased, then know that there are small bony fragments.
And it is necessary to probe the wound with a sounder, and if you find that some of those fragments are loose, then extract them and remove them. And whatever is not loose will cause pain in the limb and create discomfort, so cut it off and extract it in every way possible.
If the wound shows signs of gangrene or abscesses or another type of putrefaction and inflammation, then it is necessary to confront each of these signs with the treatment that we have presented and described in its chapter.
And it is necessary to beware of stopping at the joint and to fix your mind on it, if you have fractured a large bone, such as the femur or the humerus and their like from the major limbs, so do not neglect its treatment nor delay it. Many cases of this type are presented where death occurs, but it is delayed until the patient becomes worn out, and then he often dies twenty or thirty days after the accident.
Sometimes you may find a suitable place to treat the wound, but if not, leave it alone.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On the treatment of complications that arise after some fractures.
Many cases of this complication arise after fractures, especially near the joints, and it may even disfigure the limb or deprive it of its natural function.
I have tried and found that if the complication is mild, I use medications that constrict, such as sabur, laban, mar, anzurot, aqaqiya, and their likes, taking some or all of them and mixing them with a constricting drink or egg white or vinegar.
And apply it to the complication with a compress, and bind it tightly on it, leaving the binding for several days, then removing it and repeating it until the complication disappears, God willing. Or, you may bind onto it a poultice made of solidified lead.
For lead has a property that dispels every evil that occurs in the organs. If the complication has become entrenched and intensified, and the inflammation has moved to its vicinity, then make an incision above it and cut off the affected part or ligate it with some of the ligatures until it disappears, and treat the wound until it heals.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
On the treatment of fractures when they are consolidated and the limb remains weak after that, not returning to its original condition.
If a fracture consolidates and the limb remains weak and feeble afterwards, that is because of many reasons. Some of these reasons are the abundance of humors, the binding of the joints in an unnatural way, or the excessive tightness of the bindings, until the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or the abundance of unnecessary movement, or the movements made at the wrong time, or the scarcity of blood in the body of the patient, which leads to weakness.
Here is the translation: The nourishment is restored in it and returns to its natural shape.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of a fractured bone, if it was set with a bandage and its action was delayed until what is desired.
When a member is presented for treatment after it has been set following a fracture or dislocation, or the bone was broken and its shape was altered. Except that the member did not lose its natural function, so it is not acceptable to accept the statement of those who claim that the member was broken from its root, and many ignorant physicians and surgeons in our country have done that.
And this action is very blameworthy, leading to great harm and corrupting the treatment, but if the dislocation and breakage were slight, it would be acceptable to use the water in which the crushed herbs such as wormwood, colocynth, and king's cilantro have been soaked.
And it is treated with crushed herbs such as diachylon, or the root of wormwood is taken and pounded with chicken fat and rose oil, and it is applied with it, or the figs of Dasmur are taken and crushed with the sedge of the bath, and similar medications that are called analgesics.
And when the breakage is treated with the constant gentle traction that is done with a bandage, and the movement of the member is attempted in all directions at all times, if the dislocation has become strong and stubborn and requires the application of iron for its treatment.
It is necessary to split it open and release the connection of the bone, and to cut what remains of the breakage or bone with gentle sections, and to use gentleness in that with effort and care, then to treat the wound with what was mentioned previously until it heals.
The Thirty-Third Section.
The tonsil is a displacement of a joint from its position, which hinders movement and deforms the shape of the limb, causing severe pain and aching. When someone suffers from tonsilitis, it is necessary to hasten to treat it in order to return it to its place.
One should not delay, for if he delays, the swelling of the affected area will increase and it will be more difficult to return the tonsil to its place. Therefore, one should not delay, nor should he hesitate, nor should he be sluggish in treating it, because it often causes severe pain and aching.
But if it happens, one should hasten to bleed the affected area, then let it settle until the swelling subsides a little, and then pour warm water and oil on the limb, and finally return it to its place with gentle manipulation, treating each limb according to what is appropriate for it in its position.
And I have also arranged the sections on tonsilitis according to what has been mentioned above, from the top of the body to its lowest part.
The Forty-Second Section.
On treating tonsilitis of the lower jaw.
Do not let the tonsils dislocate except in a gentle and gradual way, for they may be on one of two surfaces, either slipping out of their position with a slight relaxation, or completely dislocating with a complete relaxation until they settle. Towards the chest, until the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it back, nor can he bear to move his jaw and articulate with his tongue, for if its dislocation is slight, it often returns to its original position with minimal effort.
And if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its return with speed and not delay, and that is by having the servant hold the patient's head and the doctor inserts his finger into the mouth on the base of the jaw, inside the cheek, if the dislocation is on one side.
He should enter the fingers of his hand, if the fracture is from both sides and the rest of his fingers are outside, he wraps them with it and orders the patient to bend his elbow and let him go to each direction, and the physician wraps the fracture and pushes it until it returns to its position.
If it is difficult to reduce, and it does not return to its position even if both fractures are complete, then use the compress with warm water and fat until it becomes easy to reduce them, and do not delay reducing them, as we have mentioned. If they return to their position and settle, and the patient's mouth is not dislocated, then at that time put on them a splint of leather strips made from tanned skin and fat.
Then bind it with a bandage, and let the patient sleep on his side with his hand elevated between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right or left, nor does he strain at all, but rather keeps still, and makes his food light.
Until, when the pain goes away and the fracture is consolidated, let him eat what he wishes, and do not use a bandage, nor should he hurry to open his mouth while eating, drinking, or talking, until the fracture is consolidated and healed.
If it is difficult to reduce the fractures, and they do not return to their positions at the right time, but rather become dislocated, then many bad consequences occur, including chronic pains and constant aches, and perhaps the patient's belly becomes swollen, or perhaps he suffers from severe and frequent relapses. If you see that, then know that it is a failure.
And many die within ten days due to this condition.
Chapter 25.
On reducing a fracture of the wrist and forearm.
As for the wrist, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest, and it dislocates outwardly, which is apparent to the sense, and its treatment is to lay the patient on his back and extend his arm.
Then press the area with your finger, pressing firmly, and then apply the bandage and the compress, and tighten it. As for its end that is near the collar bone and connects to it, do not loosen it except on the day when it should be loosened, for it may become inflamed and return to what we have mentioned.
Then apply the bandage and compress, and tighten it, and order the patient to rest in bed until he recovers. With this treatment, specifically, the end of the collar bone is treated if it has also deviated from its position.
Chapter 26.
In the treatment of a fractured collar bone.
Know that the collar bone is fractured in three ways: one of them is that it fractures towards the armpit and downwards, the second is that it fractures towards the chest, and perhaps it fractures above the collar bone, and that is during the recovery period.
It does not fracture towards the back of the shoulder, nor does it fracture towards the front of the nerve, and most often it fractures and exits downwards towards the armpit. And that is especially in those whose humerus is short, because it exits quickly in them and enters quickly.
As for those whose humerus is long, it is the opposite, meaning that it exits slowly and enters slowly. And perhaps some people suffer a blow or fall that causes the collar bone to become inflamed, and the doctor thinks that it has fractured, so it is necessary to examine it carefully until its soundness is confirmed.
So when you advance to its treatment, and you examine the fracture if it is downwards towards the armpit, you will find that the fractured collar bone and the healthy one have a clear difference. You will find that the head of the collar bone is deformed and under the armpit, upon palpation, you will find that the head of the collar bone is like an egg.
The patient cannot raise his arm to his ear, nor can he make all the necessary movements, and if he bends towards his chest or upwards, you will find that obvious for the lesion which is not hidden.
And this fracture can be reduced if it was a recent one or the patient was a young boy, and you make your assistant raise his arm above, then you will bend your fingers under his armpit and lift the joint with force upwards to its position, and the assistant raises his arm and stretches it above.
Then he puts it down below, and if it does not return quickly as we mentioned, and the fracture was several days old, it is advisable to bathe the patient in hot water and use a poultice that is relaxed and soft, like one made from the roots of plants, and the King's plaster, and it is used in the water.
Then the patient lies on his back and a ball of cotton is placed under his armpit, being moderate between softness and firmness, then the doctor puts his finger on the ball and holds the patient's arm with his hand and stretches it downwards.
And he presses the ball against his side, and if it does not return to its position, and you want to reduce it in this way, which is to prepare a longer splint than the patient's leg and attach it from the side of the thigh and insert its end under the patient's armpit and raise his arm above until the patient is suspended in the air. And another assistant pulls the patient's arm downwards to his abdomen, and if the patient is weak, it is advisable to hang another thing on him to hold him fast, so that the fracture returns to its position, and it may also be reduced in another way.
And it is that you fix a long, straight stick in the ground, its head being rounded in shape like a pestle, not too thick nor too thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on the head of the stick and the patient stands upright along the length of the stick.
Then, you should extend it to the bottom of the other side and also extend its base from the other side with strength, for the joint will quickly return to its position. If the return is difficult, use this treatment: take a splint about two fingers long. And shape it to be four fingers wide and two fingers thick, with a rounded head, so that it is easy to insert into the armpit in this manner.
Then, tie a smooth ligature on the rounded head so that it does not hurt the splint. Then, place it under the armpit and extend the entire arm onto the splint down to the bottom, and tie the splint to the upper arm and forearm, and the extremity of the hand.
Then, place the forearm on a pillow. Straighten it out and extend the arm down to the bottom, and let the rest of the body hang from the other side, for the joint will enter its position. If it is possible to enter it in this way, it may be placed under the armpit with a moderate-sized cushion.
Then, he should carry the bandaged arm with the cotton and egg whites on all the joints as it rotates from above, then tighten the cushion firmly under the armpit, and fasten the bandage on top of the arm, and suspend his hand to his neck.
And let him not move his arm for seven days, and it is desirable to make the splint's nutrition a little until the member becomes strong, for it will be quicker to heal. Then, remove it after seven or five days and exercise it with movement; if it has stuck and did not become loose, it has healed.
If the joint was dislocated frequently due to weakness or another cause, it is desirable to use the method of three splints as previously mentioned in the chapter on dislocations. If you do all this and remove the bandage after seven days, but the joint did not stabilize.
Here is the translation of the excerpt:
When the fracture is near the chest and the ribs, and towards the back of the patient, it should be treated with bandaging and medication until it heals. All harsh treatments and remedies are to be used until it mends. If after healing, a swelling appears in the limb and movement becomes difficult...
The patient should be made to use the warm bath repeatedly until that swelling subsides and he returns to his original nature.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of elbow fracture.
The elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and also returns with difficulty, and it does not dislocate to all directions, nor specifically to the front or to the back, and its fracture is not hidden from you, for it occurs under observation and under palpation in any shape of dislocation.
And if you find the elbow joint fractured and in its correct position, it will be clear to you by the apparent curvature of the joint, and he will not be able to bend his arm nor move it from its prominence, and it is necessary that you should reduce and set the fracture quickly before it becomes complicated and inflammation occurs.
For if it becomes complicated and inflammation occurs, it is difficult to reduce it, and perhaps the patient will not heal, especially if the fracture is at the back, for it is the most difficult of all types of fractures and the most painful, and often death occurs with it.
And you should force it, if possible, to return to its position, by extending his arm with the palm of your hand and his forearm bent, while the doctor's hand is above the elbow and below it, and he is pushing the joint with the fingertips of his hand all together or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its original position.
And if the fracture is at the front, it can be reduced by bending the arm once until it meets the base of the palm, which is opposite to it. If the fracture does not return to its position, then a strong and very forceful extension should be used, which is to extend the arm with great strength.
The bandage is also put on the limbs when they are dislocated, and then it is extended to each side after being wrapped around the limb with a rolled or folded compress; and if the doctor's hands are placed on the joint, he should massage it gently so that the joint may be restored to its proper place with ease.
Then the joint is manipulated strongly until it returns to its place, and after its return, it is necessary to apply the compress that contains vinegar and rose water and which has been cooled with ice, and to bandage the limb from the armpit of the arm and to leave it for a while.
Then, if the joint is fixed in its position, the bandage is removed from it and left alone. And if you see that the joint has not been restored properly, you should reapply the compress and bandage and leave it for a while until it is restored, then remove it. If pain occurs after the joint's return and removal, ...
And if there is difficulty in movement, the treatment should be done in the bath and with gentle massage and kneading until it becomes soft or makes the joint supple like a sole of leather. Then, bandage it and leave it for a day and a night, then remove it and put it in the bath. If it becomes inflamed, treat the joint with a moderate treatment.
Then, repeat the treatment on it several times, along with bathing, until it becomes soft. And if you want to make a poultice of cow's dung, mix it with rose water and oil, and apply it to the joint, doing that repeatedly, so that it becomes soft and returns to its original condition.
Chapter 28
On the Treatment of Fractures of the Clavicle
The clavicle is frequently fractured and its fracture is easy, unlike other joints, but it is necessary to hasten the reduction of the fracture at the time of fracture before the place becomes swollen or an abscess forms.
Place the surgeon's thumb on the wrist and bend it, and let the doctor place his fingers on the joint and bend them until they return to their original position, for it is necessary to examine whether the fracture has returned to its proper alignment.
Let him place the outer aspect of his hand on the wrist during flexion and extension, and if the fracture is displaced to the outer aspect of the hand, let him place the inner aspect of his hand on the wrist so that the doctor's hand falls on the same joint; if it returns to its position, then secure it with a suitable dressing for the swelling.
Do not leave it without dressing, for it will not be stable and you will not be able to reduce it after a few days have passed, unless the joint remains in its place and does not harm the hand, except that the hand has been stretched out and you were unable to grasp anything.
Therefore, you should know that the nerve has been severed or crushed, and there is no remedy for it except to use a dressing; perhaps it may be beneficial, and perhaps it will not benefit at all. If the fracture has been reduced, then put on the dressing that we have described, and secure it and leave it for five days.
Then remove and exercise the hand, for you have restricted its movement and presented something of stiffness in it; so soak it in warm water and massage it repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of fracture of the fingers.
The fingers may be fractured in any direction, so when a finger is fractured to the outer or inner aspect of the hand, bend the finger and reduce the fracture with your thumb until it returns to its position, then bind the top of the finger and suspend it towards the direction from which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and bend it until it becomes straight on that day. If it is at night, bind it in the same way, and do not cease to loosen it during the day, exercise its movement, and bind it at night; do that for several days until it becomes stable.
And you should do the same to it if you had detached it from inside the arm, and attach it to its own site, and deal with it as you dealt with it at first, until it heals. And also, deal with it similarly whenever you detach it to other sites.
Chapter Thirty.
Treatment of fracture of the vertebral column.
When a fracture occurs in the vertebral column of the back or neck, and the vertebrae are completely dislocated from their positions, there is no treatment for it, because death quickly approaches the patient.
And the sign of that is that the patient's breath comes out without control, and often some of his limbs become paralyzed, either his legs or his arms or one of them. And if one vertebra is dislocated from its position, it often becomes dislocated.
And its dislocation is to four directions, and the one that dislocates backwards is called a curvature. And as for its treatment, it is that you should examine, if the curvature has occurred from birth, there is no treatment for it, nor any remedy for it; and as for the one that occurs from a fall or a blow, and so on.
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy discourse that does not return much benefit, and I have shortened from that what is sufficient to be of little benefit from much of what they have brought together for clarification and explanation.
I say that the curvature that occurs from birth in the chest, there is no remedy for it, nor any treatment for it. And likewise, the one that occurs to the two sides as well. But I do treat the one that occurs in the back specifically, and that is by placing the patient on his face.
On a mattress, laid out on a level surface near a wall, and spread underneath it a soft cloth, so that he does not hurt his chest. Then you place a straight stick, fixed in a hole in the ground at one end of the mattress, near his head, and another stick at the other end of the mattress, near his legs.
The servant holds the scalp and another servant holds the other one in that position, then wraps around the patient's chest and under his armpits with soft and tight bandages, and extends the end of the bandage to the scalp at the head and ties it there.
Then he ties another bandage above his hips and above his knees, and at his ankles, then gathers all the bandages together and ties them to the other scalp at his legs, then each servant pulls their direction of the scalp with the bandage, without loosening the two scalps from their fixed positions.
But they are not fixed as we have mentioned, and the doctor places his palms on the scalp with force until it returns or he puts a spatula on it, then he presses on the spatula with his knees until it returns; if it does not return with this treatment, he takes a spatula whose length is approximately three cubits.
And he makes an incision in the scalp we mentioned, near the patient's chest, a place where he inserts the end of the spatula, then he puts the middle of the spatula on the prominence and the doctor places his legs on the other end and pulls strongly until the scalp is pulled out.
And it returns to its place, and if you want to secure it with a wooden board that is tied with a rope, which is to insert into the ground at the head of the patient, at the end of the two scalpels, being a cubit in length, and also at his legs, at the end of the two scalpels.
And there should be a separation of a finger's breadth between each scalp, and a hole is made in each scalp through which the wooden board passes, and all the wood is firmly secured in the ground, so that the patient does not move at all, and a curved rod is inserted, which is the wooden board on which the bandage winds around the two scalps.
On the side of the wound that has been sewn with a long suture, as is done in the two last mentioned limbs, then tighten the bandage that was applied to the chest at the joint near his head, and the bandages that were applied to his legs at the joints near his feet.
Then, a servant stands at each joint, twisting the bandage with the twisted thing that is twined around the joint, and the doctor makes the pressure on what we have mentioned, and this is the picture of the joints and the bandages and the chest.
Then, after the suture is completed and the area is settled, it is necessary to apply the compress, which is a compacted cotton pad, then the splint, and then place on top of the compress a small cushion made of leather, its width being three fingers or thereabouts, and its length being suitable for the area of the pressure.
And on some of the correct sutures, then bind with the necessary bandage, and use a light diet until he recovers. If there remains some numbness in the area after recovery, it is necessary to use the medications that soften and relax, along with the application of the leather cushion we described at length.
And a copper plate may be used for that, and numbness may appear in the last sutures of the back, and it is feared that it will become a superfluous bone, and that would be a bad consequence, so do not neglect him with this treatment lest death occur.
Chapter 31.
On the Treatment of Hernias.
Know that the joint of the groin and the joint of the shoulder, only dislocation occurs to them, and nothing else occurs to them from the lesser injuries and sprains, and the joint of the groin dislocates in four ways.
And that is because it dislocates inward, outward, forward, and backward, and most often it dislocates inward, and rarely it dislocates forward or backward. And the sign of its dislocation inward is that if you compare the healthy limb with the diseased one, it will be longer.
The fracture of the kneecap is more severe than the straight one, and the patient cannot bend his knee when it is in a position that is above the knee, and the location that is above the knee becomes swollen and painful before the head of the thigh bone has reached that point.
And the sign that indicates to him that his knee is displaced outward is that he stretches his leg on the mattress, but he does not bend it without pain being in the kneecap; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot do so until it is reduced.
And he feels heaviness in his knee and numbness in his leg, and when walking, it is as if he has a weight on his hip; and the sign that indicates to him that his knee is displaced backward is that he cannot stretch his kneecap nor bend it before bending his leg, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the leg is relaxed and the head of the thigh bone is at the position of the buttock, and as for the return of this type of fracture, it is that you should look: if the fracture was old and had adhered to its companion and did not try to reduce it, and it remained in its condition, then there is no treatment for it.
It is not advisable to show it to him, and as for the one whose fracture is recent and was of any type among the four types of fractures, you should hasten to manipulate the joint, stretching it inward and outward, and moving it gently from side to side.
If it does not return to its place with no benefit from treatment, then if it does not return in this way, it is advisable to put strong wooden splints on his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied above the knee and another splint that extends from above.
By inserting his hands from under his armpit, then tightening the bandage to settle on the basis of the thigh, and holding the end of the splint with another splint, which is the third, and it extends either from the front near the knee to the hip, or from behind to the back.
And they bandage each of them with a single bandage until the swelling goes down and the limb remains suspended, for this type of bandaging is a common type for the four categories. If you return to what we mentioned earlier, then it's not necessary for each category to have a specific treatment.
But the specific return, if the fracture is inward, it is necessary to place the swelling on its correct aspect, then to apply compresses at the base of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the point below the pubic area.
Then the bandage is extended from below the pubic area to above the abdomen, up to the region of the navel, then another strong servant takes hold with his fingers and presses on the thick part of the thigh, and tightly bandages outward, so that it returns to its position.
And this type is easier than other types of treatment that involve the return of this member, so you should not hesitate and should not be reluctant to enter into this type of treatment, for it is necessary to bandage the entire limb with a strong bandage, so that it rests on the ankles and knees.
And each one is at a distance of four fingers from its companion, and the upper limb is extended more than the other by two fingers, then the limb is suspended on a wooden beam that is in the house, and it is at a distance of two cubits from the ground.
Then you order a strong servant to hold the head of the thigh and suspend it with another servant, and the other servant holding the thigh with strength, so that the joint returns to its position quickly. But the specific return, if the fracture is outward.
It is necessary to place the limb on the pillows according to what we described in the owner of the curvature, and to tighten the bandage on his upper limb specifically and on his chest, then to place one wooden beam at his ankles and another at his head, then to place an additional wooden beam in the middle of the pillows, strongly secured.

Chapters Two and Three.
On the treatment of fractures of the elbow.
The elbow is dislocated in three ways: inward, outward, and downward, which means toward the back, and not forward. The sign of its fracture is that the upper arm is unable to flex its thigh towards its hip.
If the bandage does not stick to the thigh, then know that the knee is fractured, and reset all its facets so that the patient lies down on his back with his leg stretched out, and if it is strong enough for that, he should lie down with his leg bent behind him, and his hip should be slightly turned towards the back.
And stick your back to his face, and put his leg between yours, then bind his knee with your palms, and interlace them with your fingers on his knee, then constrict your palms on both sides of his knee with strength, and another servant stretches out his leg until the knee returns to its position.
And the sign of its return is that you stick the leg to the thigh in a straight line without deviation, then constrict it and stick the leg to the thigh, then bind them both together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them, and do not use it except for a little walking every day until it gains strength.
If you are unable to return it with what we have described, then use strong stretching with the bandages that we have mentioned and described for treating fractures, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of ankle fracture.
The ankle may become dislocated, and it may break on the calf, and its fracture may be either inward or outward, and the sign of its fracture is that you see the ankle swollen and deformed towards the side to which it was broken.
So, the treatment of its dislocation is to stretch it gently with the hands until it returns, and if it breaks on the calf, it should be treated by lying down on his back with a strong servant holding him from behind his back in his middle.
Then you hold with your right hand his foot from above, and with your left hand from below the foot at the ankle joint, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand, twice, then stretch it with your left hand and push the upper part of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without twisting.
Make it firm as we described, then insert the sole of the foot into the leg in the third position, and you will find that it has been bent with the ankle, but if you return to this position or make it so twice, you will see the foot is straightened, unless you repeat the process on it, for it will revert.
For if you are unable to restore it as we described, then put the patient on his back on the ground and thrust strongly, making sure to place it between his thighs, and wrap a bandage around it so that the patient does not feel discomfort, then the servant will hold his thigh.
Then another servant will stretch out the man's leg, either with his hands or with a bandage to bind it to the patient's neck, then each servant will pull in the opposite direction of his companion and stand upright between the patient's thighs, holding him so that he does not jerk his body downward when stretched, then the physician will set the fracture with his hands.
And another servant will hold the healthy leg downwards, for the fracture will quickly return to its place. If the fracture returns and you see that it has returned to its proper position, then apply the pad and splint, and tie them with bandages and straps, and bind the foot with a bandage downwards, and beware of the nerve that is above the ankle from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight on it, then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage becomes loose, tighten it and release it on the third or fourth day. And the patient will refrain from walking for forty days.
For if he walks before this period, it is feared that the fracture will be dislocated and become worse, and it will not accept treatment afterwards. So, it is necessary to warn him of a severe penalty, and it is necessary to use in calming him what we have presented and described in another place of treatment and restraint until he recovers.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of fracture of the fingers of the hand.
It is necessary to straighten what has been bent from it with a slight extension without exaggeration, and it should not be difficult but rather easy. If the fracture was in some of the toes of the foot, it is necessary to put the bandage on a level place on the ground.
Or on a board, and stand like a masseur, then put your foot on what you want from those joints, and press it with your foot forcefully until it returns to its place and appears in its original position without deviation.
Then put under the sole of his foot a board that takes the entire foot, being raised at both ends, and bind it tightly for three days, then remove it and prevent him from walking for many days until he recovers and is safe from relapse, God willing.
Chapter 35.
On the types of fracture that occur with a wound, or with a bruise, or with both altogether.
When something like this happens and its treatment is neglected, it often leads to death.
And therefore, it is not proper for anyone to undertake the treatment of such a case unless he is experienced in the craft, has had long practice, is gentle, cautious, and careful, not hasty or negligent, and uses in the beginning only medications that calm the painful swellings and adjust the bandage.
You should not hasten to do anything for the patient until you have ensured his safety from the injury, with a lightness of the disease and a sign of hope appearing in it, so that you return him to health from the first hour before the painful swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to what you desired, then use the management that calms the painful swellings and treat the wound with what benefits it from dry poultices. If the fracture was with a bruise and there were splinters in the bone, then remove them. We have mentioned in that which we stated concerning the simple wounds what is presented in their locations.
Exert your effort and free yourself from entering into the way of ignorance according to what you have presented and arranged for yourself, thus it will remain for your sake and be safe for your presentation, if God so wills.
